It will require maritime navigation<span id="sidebarnotebase"><<insert "<sup>&angsph;</sup>">><div id="sidebarnote"><sup>&angsph;</sup> Without, of course, any instrumentation. Instead, I'll answer a different question, one that you might have asked, eventually.</p><p>This is a game, or rather a hub for a suite of smaller games. These games have been built in the [[Twine engine|http://twinery.org]], an open-source software used to create primarily text adventures. In these adventures, the reader, or player, reads their way through a story by making choices. These choices, in their turn, decide how the story unfolds and what comes next. There are [[many|http://www.depressionquest.com/]] [[excellent|http://correlatedcontents.com/misc/Father.html]] [[examples|http://aliendovecote.com/uploads/twine/LD26/ruin.html#1]], in every genre, built using this software.</p><p>This particular clutch of Twine games were created by myself, [[Rob Sherman|http://bonfiredog.co.uk]], a writer, game designer and musician. Under duress, this is about as lucid as you'll ever find me. I am currently the Interactive Writer-In-Residence at the [[British Library|http://bl.uk]], and will be until March 2015. I am in the process of creating an artistic reaction, both physical, digital, petulant and grown-up, to the //[[Lines In The Ice|http://www.bl.uk/events/lines-in-the-ice-seeking-the-northwest-passage]]// exhibition at the Library. The exhibition itself is free, and you should come and visit it; you will find some of the components of my work there, on display and with texture and dimensions and everything. These games are another component of this larger work that I am creating, collectively titled //On My Wife's Back//. The project's blog can be found [[here|http://onmywifesback.tumblr.com]], with a more rigourous key to its constituent parts. Some of these constituents can, as I have written, be found in the gallery space, while others can only be experienced online or by meeting me and hearing me expel sound in person. However, if you cannot come to the Library, or to me, because of a rather-embarrassing bout of Anglical Impairment<span id="sidebarnotebase"><<insert "<sup>&angsph;</sup>">><div id="sidebarnote"><sup>&angsph;</sup> Or, to put it another way, you have absolutely no intention of visiting the United Kingdom any time soon.</div><<endinsert>></span>, be reassured that as much of the //On My Wife's Back// content as possible, in all forms, will eventually be digitised and made freely available worldwide.</p><p>So, to answer that question that you did not ask, these are some games, part of a much larger fictional work attached to an exhibition at the British Library.</p><<endreplace>>\n<div id="aboutbodyhr"><img src="img/hr1.png" alt="horizontal rule" /></div>\n\n<<replace>><div id="aboutbodytitle">What Are The Games About?</div><<becomes>><div id="aboutbodytitle">What Are The Games About?</div>\n<p>For that, I'll need to tell you a little about the exhibition.</p><p>//Lines In The Ice// tells the story, and displays the artefacts, of the relationship between the Arctic regions and the personal and professional imaginations of mankind, mostly amongst the Britons both Great and pathetic. It shows the many attempts to traverse all those forbidding, fairy-winking places down the centuries which, despite their deadliness and cultural chastity, have made Europeans and North Americans horribly excited for generations. As you will see if you come to the exhibition, these attempts have all been fairly soft-headed, but there is one that stands out as particularly doughy; the 1845 expedition of Sir John Franklin to navigate the Northwest Passage. You can read more about John Franklin [[here|https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Franklin]], but I should give a short summary of his glories and his clownings. A famed captain of 59 when he set sail, he disappeared into the Arctic wilderness, leaving behind only a mounting legend of his whereabouts, some oral mythology amongst those Inuit which he came into contact with, and a littered trail of rusted tin cans, strewn across a hundred beaches of the far North. Since then, there have been almost-endless attempts to locate his remains, the remains of his crew, and the wrecks of his ships. These attempts, equally soft-headed, began some 2 years after Sir Franklin left, and continue up to the present day<span id="sidebarnotebase"><<insert "<sup>&angsph;</sup>">><div id="sidebarnote"><sup>&angsph;</sup> In fact, just two months after I began my residency at the Library, an expedition funded by the Canadian government discovered Franklin's flagship, the HMS //Erebus//, at the bottom of the Victoria Strait. Despite the geopolitical folksiness of Prime Minister Steven Harper, few people seemed to know how to react; we are, after all, not Victorians anymore.</div><<endinsert>></span>.</p><p>I am, by and large, less interested in the facts of this history, apart from the ways in which they can inform my own artistic work. //On My Wife's Back//, therefore, takes Franklin's expedition and the cozy years surrounding it as its habitat, and into this habitat inserts one extremely fictional man; [[Isaak Scinbank|http://onmywifesback.tumblr.com/scinbank]].</p><p>According to nobody but myself, Scinbank was a Derbyshire man born and bred, the son of Britain's first paediatrician, a passionate angler and, apparently, a decorated sailor. Though little record, even in my fictional universe, remains of such decoration, it seems that his reputation was such that he was commissioned by John Franklin's then-maybe-widow, Lady Jane Franklin, to travel up into the Arctic and its impossible archipelago of Wales-sized islands<span id="sidebarnotebase"><<insert "<sup>&angsph;</sup>">><div id="sidebarnoteright"><sup>&angsph;</sup> Because everything in geography, it seems, must be either bigger or smaller than the meridian of Wales.</div><<endinsert>></span> to find her husband, or at least a kowtowable portion of his corpse. Beginning in November 1852, Scinbank journeyed first to the Orkney islands, then Hudson's Bay to hibernate through to 1853, and then finally high up into the sea ice, facing all those vague, glorious threats which threatened Victorian seamen; death, deprivation, starvation, mutiny, disease and hallucination. In my version of events, this is where I start to cleave from the history. Scinbank finds Franklin, brings him home, and is promptly forgotten in all the excitement. Great Britain, it seems, had far too much to celebrate.</p><p>This fading is of course not Scinbank's fault; he is, after all, fictional. His day had to wait until the //Lines In The Ice// exhibition, and my residency, when I had the occasion to invent him and remember him, all at once. After inventing him, I had to create the scattered, artfully decayed evidence that he was ever alive. I am writing, on his behalf, his own diaries, kept onboard ship whilst in the Arctic and included in the exhibition as if they had been interred in the Library's archives all along. I am composing the traditional music that might have been sung by his sailors as they hauled rope. I am plotting the bespoke constellations, known only to his ancestors, which he used to navigate the heavens<span id="sidebarnotebase"><<insert "<sup>&angsph;</sup>">><div id="sidebarnoteright"><sup>&angsph;</sup> Remember the Rod, and its turpentine arc in the sky?</div><<endinsert>></span>. I am drawing his drawings, and painting his paintings. I am even sourcing, constructing and patina-ing objects and artefacts which might have belonged to him, which might have survived him, and which might have meant an awful lot to him. All of these different pieces of work, in all these different mediums, combine to become //On My Wife's Back//.</p><p>However, despite its seeming breadth, the story told between all these different components is by necessity only as 'history' has preserved it. In the fiction, these are the fragments which have survived, have been preserved, and which are considered sexy and relevant enough to be included in Scinbank's official narrative, a narrative which suits a public exhibition such as the one to which I am attached. In creating this account I have tried to simulate the way in which such accounts become unassailable, murky and serpentine as they get older and more institutionalised. Like any old story, its author is no longer alive to verify it, nor are any of those people with whom he came into contact and who might be able to give conflicting accounts. Apart from his sea-journal and a few other elements, very little survives of his travels in the Arctic, and so despite its enormous gaps, inconsistencies and unspokens, the story told is historically unimpeachable. Visitors to the Library, and the blog, can only read the diary, look at the objects, study the [[official biographies|http://onmywifesback.tumblr.com/scinbank]] and draw what limited conclusions they can. There is little recognizance that history may have obliterated some of the more interesting parts of the story, or even that Scinbank and others may have censored it to suit public consumption<span id="sidebarnotebase"><<insert "<sup>&angsph;</sup>">><div id="sidebarnote"><sup>&angsph;</sup>Many accounts by explorers at this time were published in some form or another. Being a Victorian gentleman, you can only imagine what internal strictures he might place upon himself when writing, or expurgating.</div><<endinsert>></span>.</p><p>These games, then, stand outside this 'official' narrative of Isaak Scinbank as presented by me at the British Library and elsewhere online. By playing these games, you may receive more visceral, more honest and perhaps more truthful accounts of what happened to him and those around him, both throughout his life and deep in that 'thunderless Cloudbank' at the pate of the world.</p><<endreplace>>\n\n<div id="aboutbodyhr"><img src="img/hr2.png" alt="horizontal rule" width="400px" height="23px" /></div>\n<<replace>><div id="aboutbodytitle">How Do I Play?</div><<becomes>><div id="aboutbodytitle">How Do I Play?</div>\n<p>All of the games are accessed through the main page from which you have just come. The curious object that you find centred there is a cache baton; a hollow cylinder of copper often taken to the Arctic by explorers. It was used as a waterproof, chemically-hardy method of leaving messages on important, lonely, shores, marking a particular expedition's passing, the weather, the state of their health, and anything else a fellow, inquisitive Englishman might wish to know, if he ever came across it. These batons were often buried under cairns of rock, and some survived to be found by less-Imperial, less-English explorers<span id="sidebarnotebase"><<insert "<sup>&angsph;</sup>">><div id="sidebarnote"><sup>&angsph;</sup>See [[Lobsticks And Stone Cairns: Human Landmarks In The Arctic|http://www.amazon.co.uk/Lobsticks-Stone-Cairns-Landmarks-Arctic/dp/1895176697]], edited by Richard C. Davis.</div><<endinsert>></span>. The example depicted on the main page is a baton left behind by Isaak Scinbank on his travels; you can see the mark of its maker along the side. It has been denuded of its cairn, accumulating snow by the minute whilst not-so-feeble bacteria poignantly, regretfully chart verdigris across its surface. You had best answer the question it asks you, its finder, before it is buried completely. Can you read it? </p>\n\n<div id="wheregone">Wher&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;ave&nbsp;You&nbsp;Gon&nbsp;?</div>\n\n<p>You see, each game takes place in a location from //On My Wife's Back//; this may be a place from Isaak Scinbank's life, either from the world he resides in and the world which resides in him, or maybe something mentioned by one of his sailors in song. If a location appears in //On My Wife's Back//, either in Scinbank's journal, the blog, this Twine game or even incised onto the physical artefacts of Scinbank which I have in my possession<span id="sidebarnotebase"><<insert "<sup>&angsph;</sup>">><div id="sidebarnote"><sup>&angsph;</sup>And which I might one day be persuaded to photograph.</div><<endinsert>></span>, there is a possibility that it may have a game attached to it. The only way to find out is to input the location right into the box on the baton's surface and click the button. If you pick a 'correct' location, you will be taken directly to the corresponding game. If you are incorrect, you will be brought back to the baton, and its howling field. The locations always take the form of <strong>proper placenames or co-ordinates of latitude and longitude</strong>. You must enter them into the main page <strong>exactly as you find them</strong>. They are extremely case-and-context-sensitive. For example:</p>\n<ul><li>With actual placenames, check whether they are capitalised, whether clauses are separated<span id="sidebarnotebase"><<insert "<sup>&angsph;</sup>">><div id="sidebarnote"><sup>&angsph;</sup>For example, there is such minute difference between 'Milldale', 'Mill Dale' and 'mill Dale'.</div><<endinsert>></span>, and whether they are followed by any punctuation which may be an integral part of the placename itself;</li>\n<li>When entering co-ordinates, be sure that they are correctly formatted. Co-ordinates will always be formatted as below, to one decimal place.</li></ul>\n<div id="wheregone2">45.2N, -30.5W</div>\n\n<p>Once you do discover a correct location, and it leads you to a game, traversal should be fairly intuitive. Most of the games are text-based, and so literacy and a functioning digit to click the mouse is all you really need. Each game works on a similar principle; read what is on-screen, and then make a choice from the available options. By clicking an option, which looks like <<replace "this...">>this, something in the story will change, either on the current page or by being whisked to another part of the story. There may be other mechanics and features in individual games, other things to click or press or manipulate, which might be explained if it is narratively expedient. A profound literacy //of games// and how they function should not be required to play, only an open mind and an acceptance of the fact that you probably will not be able to circumnavigate them entirely, which is my fault and not yours. You will not be able to choose all choice, read all the words, or know Isaak's story entirely. Even I don't know everything about him, which is the way it should be.</p>\n\n<span id="backupfirstletter">T</span>hese games were created in a certain spirit. They are experimental, perhaps temperamental, but hopefully always interesting. They will reward study of the other parts of //On My Wife's Back//, perhaps not through repetition or reptilian reactions but instead by concentration, a generous mind and a breadth of exploration. They are not designed for entertainment or competition. There is unlikely to be any game which you can lose in a manner which has any meaning. They are not designed to make you feel stupid.<<endreplace>>\n\n<span id="backupfirstletter">P</span>lease keep in mind that this is an iterative project, which will be augmented continually and irregularly. Accordingly, all of its elements are in a state of flux; I will add to Scinbank's diary, create new music, and design new games as I go, and so location codes which do not work earlier on may work later. If this frustrates you, sorry. I should also point out<span id="sidebarnotebase"><<insert "<sup>&angsph;</sup>">><div id="sidebarnote"><sup>&angsph;</sup>Though you have probably gathered by this deep, deep point.</div><<endinsert>></span> that these games should work in any modern browser with CSS3 and HTML5 capabilities, on desktops, laptops and most tablets, though if you try to play them on a phone, what occurs may just serve you right.<<endreplace>>\n\n<div id="aboutbodyhr"><img src="img/hr1.png" alt="horizontal rule" width="400px" height="23px" /></div>\n<<replace>><div id="aboutbodytitle">Where Do I Go To Learn More?</div><<becomes>><div id="aboutbodytitle">Where Do I Go To Learn More?</div>\n<p>What a wonderful, impromptu question. I couldn't have written it better myself. Everything regarding my residency<span id="sidebarnotebase"><<insert "<sup>&angsph;</sup>">><div id="sidebarnote"><sup>&angsph;</sup>Which, I'm aware, might be starting to collapse under its own weight.</div><<endinsert>></span> can be found at my [[research blog|http://onmywifesback.tumblr.com]]. This is the //campus// for news, developments and research from my own work, as well as [[downloads of additional material|http://onmywifesback.tumblr.com/downloads]], [[interactive maps|http://onmywifesback.tumblr.com/map]], [[acknowledgements|http://onmywifesback.tumblr.com/crew]], [[bibliographies|http://onmywifesback.tumblr.com/biblio]] and [[information on how to submit your own ideas to the world that I am creating|http://onmywifesback.tumblr.com/submitpage]].</p><p>A</span>part from that, more information on Twine games in general is available [[at the engine's homepage|http://twinery.org]], and the Library's Digital Research department, who are supporting me with this residency, blog [[here|http://britishlibrary.typepad.co.uk/digital-scholarship/]].</p><p>Any bug reports, suggestions or otherwise can be submitted by following the advice on [[this page|http://onmywifesback.tumblr.com/submitpage]].</p><p>Are you interested in <<replace>>how this game is being made using the Twine software?</p><<becomes>>how this game is being made using Twine software? Well, you can download it from source and open it in the Twine editing software on your own computer, which will show its narrative structure and technical workings. ''Please note that this will spoil everything.'' If you are still interested, do the following:<br /><ul><li>Right-click anywhere on this page, and choose 'Save As'. The format that you want is ''.html'', otherwise called ''Webpage, Complete''.</li><br /><li>Open the Twine software on your computer '(version 1.4.2)', or [[download and install it|http://twinery.org]] if you have not.</li><br /><li>In the Twine software, click ''File->Import->Compiled HTML File...'' and navigate to the downloaded .html file. This should open the game's development structure. Please note that all of the assets (images, sound files &c.) referenced in the code are stored on the server, and are not included in the .html file.</li></ul><<endreplace>><<endreplace>><br />\n<div /%\n<<playbgm iHBWZn9re0U 32>> plays the YouTube video starting from 32 seconds in.\n*<<playbgm iHBWZn9re0U>> plays the YouTube video from the beginning, or resumes it if it was paused.\n*<<stopbgm iHBWZn9re0U>> stops the sound track. \n*<<loopbgm iHBWZn9re0U>> makes the sound track constantly loop (although it may not quite be seamless).\n*<<unloopbgm iHBWZn9re0U>> stops it from looping.\n*<<pausebgm iHBWZn9re0U>> pauses the sound track.\n*<<stopbgm iHBWZn9re0U>> stops playback and resets its position to the start.\n\n-----\n\nList of macros\nThis set includes the following "revision macros":\n* <<insert>>, <<replace>>, <<continue>> — triggered by clicking their contents.\n* <<timedreplace>>, <<timedinsert>>, <<timedremove>>, <<timedcontinue>>, <<timedcycle>> — triggered by time.\n* <<hoverreplace>>, <<hoverremove>> — triggered by the mouse touching their contents, only temporarily.\n* <<mousereplace>>, <<mouseremove>>, <<mousecontinue>>, <<mousecycle>> — triggered by the mouse touching their contents, permanently.\n* <<once>>, <<later>> — triggered by visiting the passage a certain number of times in the game session.\n* <<insertion>>, <<revision>>, <<removal>>, <<cycle>> — triggered by a separate "triggering macro".\n\nIt also includes these "triggering macros":\n* <<revise>>, <<revert>>, <<randomise>> — hyperlinks.\n* <<mouserevise>>, <<hoverrevise>> — sections that the mouse can touch.\n\nChanged syntax\n* <<replacewith>>, used to separate sections in <<timedreplace>>, has been replaced with <<becomes>>.\n* You can now have multiple sections in most of the revision macros, by separating them with <<becomes>> or <<gains>> macros:\n\nThese function just like they do within <<revision>> macros - <<becomes>> replaces the previous section with the next section, and <<gains>> merely appends the next section.\n* To reconcile differences between <<replace>> and <<timedreplace>>'s syntax, some macros (currently just the <<replace>>, <<mousereplace>> and <<hoverreplace>> varieties, as well as <<mousecontinue>>) have multiple syntax for specifying sections - the "normal" syntax, where each section is separated by <<becomes>>/<<gains>>, and a "shorthand" syntax, where the first several sections are strings within the opening tag:\n\nThe final section must be between the opening tag and the <<endreplace>> macro tag, though. \nFor most of these, the shorthand strings behave as if the <<becomes>> macro separates them. The exception to this is the "insert" named macros, wherein the strings will behave as if the <<gains>> macro separates them.\n\nNew macros\n* <<later>> is the antonym of <<once>> - it displays its contained text on the second and subsequent visits.\n* <<mousecycle>> creates a section of text that cycles between different versions whenever the mouse touches it. It's similar to <<cyclinglink>> in some respects, though you can't yet easily bind a variable to change when it changes.\n* <<timedcycle>> constantly rotates through its sections without stopping. It is similar to <<timedloop>> and may well replace it in future.\n\nExamples\n\nIn any of these examples, <<becomes>> can be substituted with <<gains>>.\n\n------\n\nFeatures\n\nIf the first parameter begins with the "$" sigil, then it will interpret it as a variable to be altered by clicking the link. When the player visits the page or clicks the link, the variable will be changed to match the text of the link. If the variable already has a string value when you load the passage, then the link text that matches the variable will be selected, instead of the first one.\n\nIf the last parameter is the word "end", then it will terminate the cycling link at the parameter before last. The containing that text will replace the link element altogether. Similarly, if the last parameter is the word "out", then it will disappear altogether after the final link text is clicked.\n\nUsage examples: \n* You look around, but only see <<cyclinglink "grass" "a flower" "a cloud" "the road">>. - This is a purely cosmetic, endlessly cycling link.\n* You see a bowl containing <<cyclinglink "3 cookies" "2 cookies" "1 cookie" "a few crumbs" end>>. - This link changes to the words "a few crumbs" when you get to the end.\n* You see a dial: <<cyclinglink $heat "off" "low" "high" "fearsome">>. - The $heat variable will be changed to "off" when the page loads (unless it was already set to "high" or "fearsome"), and then sets it to "low", "high" and "fearsome" as the player clicks the link.\n* You see a fuel gauge: <<cyclinglink $fuel "100%" "50%" "10%" "0%" end>>.\n* You see an air meter: <<cyclinglink $air "********" "******" "****" "**" out>>.\n\n----------\n\nGoogle Docs Spreadsheet - can record and fetch.\n\nThese macros are included in lalanlgdoc.twinemacro.js:\nlogdata\n\nThe logdata macro writes arbitrary text to a spreadsheet. It is write-only.\nSyntax: <<logdata name data sheet>>\nExample:<<logdata "My Name" "Some Data" "feedback">>\nLogs "My Name", "Some Data" to the spreadsheet set up as "feedback" in log.php.\n\nname: any name to identify the source of the logged data\ndata: any arbitrary data\nsheet: the spreadsheet in $google_spreadsheets in log.php\nfetch\n\nSyntax: <<fetch columnToFetch keyColumn key sheet twineResultVariable>>\nExample:<<fetch description monsterid 2 fetch1 $myMonster>>\nThe monster is <<print $myMonster>>\nFetches the description of the row with MonsterId=2 from sheet set up as "fetch1" in fetch.php and stores the result in $myMonster for use in Twine. The second statement prints the result of the first.\n\nname: any name to identify the source of the logged data\ndata: any arbitrary data\nsheet: the spreadsheet in $google_spreadsheets in log.php\n\n-------------\n\n<<timedloop 1s>>You have <<set $red += 1>><<print $red>> seed pods.<<endtimedloop>>\n\n-------------\n\n* <<timedgoto "underwater" 2.5s >> goes to the "underwater" passage if you stay at the current passage for 2 and 1/2 seconds.\n* <<timedgoto $deathpassage 5s >> goes to the passage whose name is in the $deathpassage variable if you stay at the current passage for 5 seconds.\n* <<timedgoto $playerType + "pit" 1s >> goes to the $playerType + "pit" passage if you stay at the current passage for 1 second.\n* <<goto "Throne">> - This shorter version functions the same as <<timedgoto "Throne" 0s>>.\n\nLike a living story\n\n-----------\n\n<<remember>> - punish people for other players actions, show other actions\n\n<<forget>>\n\n<<print>> variables\n\n<<actions>> preclude any they have seen, just the once\n\n<<choice>> - Just make it once.\n\n<<display>>\n\n<<print either>>\n\nrefresh the page\n\nerase text on mouseover\n\nhow many times it has been visited\n\nrevision\n\n\nRandom\n\nPrevious()\n\nVisited\n\nConfirm()\n\nPrompt()\n\nopen()\n\ntransition tag\n\n\n<<continue>>\n\nStrikethrough text\n\nSub and super script\nMonospace\nLists\nTables\n\nPaned messages\n\n%/\n
